I knew nothing, precisely nothing about Fabio before watching Fix: The Ministry Movie, a documentary of industrial metal band Ministry's hideously unpleasant 1996 "Sphinctour" tour... amongst all the band and crew struggling with severe drug addiction and a host of emotional issues while on tour, Fabio turns up (out one of Ministry knows him, Uncle Al Jourgensen seems to know a great many people). I had no idea who he was, just this super buff Italian sounding dude with long hair shows up, and everyone's like "Oh, Fabio's here?!". Such an obscure place to see this character I know nothing about, surrounded by a band who are in the process of living the best and worst of the "Rock And Roll" lifestyle, attracting celebrities, stalkers, death threats... people are having overdoses off camera, someone tear-gassed the the venue mid song (culminating in Uncle Al taking to the stage wearing a bullet proof vest, carrying a 6D cell Maglite). I still know virtually nothing about Fabio, but seeing his uncanny-valley level visage in front of a cannon that fires simulacrum dead geese is... pretty badass.
Put a vacuum actuated piston in there so all the air leaves the tank instantaneously and the projectile is able to use the whole length of the barrel to speed up. With air cannons you need to at minimum match the volume of the tank to the barrel but the higher ratio tank:barrel with the piston, the more the potential the projectile has to accelerate. I found that using a standard ball valve, the slight leak after initially breaking the valve causes a slight loss in cannon power. With the losses becoming greater the longer the valve took to open.   Beautiful video mate, hopefully my ramblings can give an idea or help inspire someone to try and build something better. Watching Mythbusters when I was little got me into engineering, made me fall in love with the scientific method.

Love this collaboration! Very fun guys. I knew exactly what happened when Adam fell in the clay. I got into some clay like that while visiting my daughter in Arizona. My son-in-law was taking us to Meteor Crater, and we got out of the car to take some pictures of the mountains. I was wearing brand new Doc Martin clogs, that have an open back, and lambs wool inside. I managed to not get the clay all the way to the wool, but that's because it was building up thicker and thicker on the bottom of my shoes. We tried and tried to scrape it off, and I even used baby wipes. It finally came off after walking and scraping on concrete for 4 hours. My son-in-law has lived in Arizona his whole life, and thought that's just always how mud is everywhere. Their house is actually right next to a hill that has 1000 year old native ruins on top. They use the mud/clay, and there are even a bunch of broken pieces of pottery. He does his based to keep an eye on it, as it isn't counted in the known Spots that the state and universities mapped out. It's no wonder the Navajo made so much beautiful pottery.

Riding through west Texas on my way to El Paso from Dallas, I saw another biker going the opposite way get hit straight in the chest by a buzzard.  I have no idea how fast the bird was going, but the rider was going at least 80mph which is the speed limit on that stretch of I-10.  Somehow, he kept his seat and was able to slow down and drop his kickstand before falling over.  I immediately turned around and got on the eastbound side. J-Lube is a classic. It's one of my go-to products. This stuff is so slippery, you have no idea. Just spill it onto a smooth floor and it will get more slippery than wet ice. It's pretty much impossible to walk on, no matter which shoes and how grippy the shoe sole may be. It's also great for pranks: Cover someone's car with it. It's very hard to get rid of that stuff. You want to wash it off? Sure, keep adding more water and make it even more slippery, but it won't go anywhere. And if you need a lubricant for "certain activities", if this lube isn't slippery enough, nothing else is. Also, when it starts to dry out, you can always add a little water and it'll be back to normal. So it's super long lasting and you only need tiny amounts of it, unless you want to make entire buckets of slime, making it actually pretty inexpensive in comparison to other products. As long as it stays dry, it won't ever spoil. Even after 10 years, it will be as good as on day one as soon as you mix some of it with water. Give it a try and you'll never want to use another lube again. And yes, it won't affect latex or any other materials. But again, serious warning: If you accidentally spill it to the ground, immediately use towels to soak it up. You could seriously hurt yourself if you slip on it.

this happened in germany with a tesla a few years back, it caught on fire and the doors wouldnt open since there is no mechanical connection...locked four 18/19year olds in the car, the driver and his girlfriend next to him managed to escape the car severly injured but the two 18 year olds in the back died, one died either at or very quickly after the crash itself, but the other was proven by autopsy to have burned to death, tesla says the door handles on the outside could stick out in the "unlikely case of a collision" but they didnt. The internal doorhandles failed since the electronics gave out immediately when the fire started, the backup mechanical unlock was located underneath the floormats...a forensic accident analyist has claimed in court that the door handles not working and the backup not being accessible due to safety belt and airbags were the cause the second one in the backseat could not be saved. they are thinking about sueing tesla for that because a normal car with a regular mechanical door handle would've allowed her to be saved from the car, her injuries wouldn't have necessarily been fatal as the autopsy report showed. the driver was arrested after his release from hospital but sentenced according to juvenile status, he got a 3 months driving ban but will only be provided his license again once he presents a positive med psych eval (~1500-2500â‚¬) and had to pay a fine as well not sure what that was

I can attest to what Adam is saying at the end about the clay. I live in northern Minnesota gray clay is everywhere. If it gets on your shoes or more appropriately your boots it will stay there, and pile up sticking to itself like gum. When I was kid there was a logging road less than a mile from my house that was basically made of the stuff. If it was dry it was fine but one day after it had rained I decided to ride my bike down it. I didn't get fifty yards before I could no longer peddle because the clay had piled up on my tires so much it was getting peeled off by the front forks, and the back frame. When I couldn't pedal anymore, and had to push the bike back to the road. It's both sticky and slippery at the same time. If you tried to drive a car down that same road when it was wet the cars tire treads would fill up with clay and then slide all over the road like you were on ice. Fun stuff. Oh yeah, once it dries, it's like concrete.

A couple of tips for the team if they want to reach higher speed with that air cannon. 1 Trim the barrel shorter. Due to the flow restriction into the barrel, the intial velocity in the barrel will build up a pressure wave in front of the goose, and the void behind the goose will reduce the pressure behind the goose in a fairly short distance. The speed profile on a launch can be recorded by launching a small magnet inside the goose and placing sensing coils at a regular interval along the barrel. I used 1 foot spacing and started with a 2 inch valve, and a 10 foot barrel. Velocity peaked and dropped off well before 10 feet. An overly long barrel does not equate to longer acceleration distance with constant acceleration. With a valve much smaller than 2 inch in that cannon with that diameter barrel, the acceleration is complete in well under 5 feet. To clock the magnet launches, used an oscilloscope to capture the classic S curve of the magnet passing. Center of the coil is in the middle of the S where the magnet is not approaching or leaving a coil. Measure the time from one crossing to the next to get the time to travel 1 foot. Directly converts to mS per Foot, which inverted is Feet/Second. Did this project as part of an engineering challenge. It was the method used to find the optimum barrel trim for a pressure and projectile.
